Rulings on Gold and Silver Vessels and Body Parts
Story and rulings on using silver and gold for vessels and prosthetics

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on using gold and silver vessels and prosthetics, and distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Core Idea
Silver vessels are disliked, but gold may be used for necessary prosthetics due to prophetic permission.

This passage opens with a famous anecdote showing Imam Abu Hanifa’s precise legal reasoning: drinking from a silver vessel is only disliked if the mouth directly touches the silver. It then expands to rulings on artificial noses and teeth, highlighting the school’s attention to necessity and the distinction between direct and indirect use. The commentary also contrasts pouring oil (disliked) with applying perfume (not disliked) from gold or silver containers, based on how the substance is used.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine the hukm level for each scenario: silver vessels, gold nose, silver nose, reattaching a tooth.
Silver vessels are makruh tanzihi; gold vessels are haram, but prosthetics have a dispensation.
2
Locate the proof
Find the dalil for each ruling. The hadith of 'Urfajah is cited for the gold nose.
The hadith is ahad, zanni in transmission and meaning, but strong in its context.
3
Note the ikhtilaf
Observe the intra-Hanafi differences: Abu Hanifa prohibits gold nose, Muhammad allows it based on the hadith; Abu Yusuf permits gold/silver for teeth.
The mufta bihi for prosthetics is permissibility of gold due to necessity.
4
Trace the reasoning
Understand Imam Abu Hanifa’s analogy of a silver ring: if the ring touches the palm while drinking, it is disliked; similarly, the vessel is disliked only when the mouth touches the silver.
This shows a nuanced approach to dislike based on direct contact.
5
Evaluate practical differences
Consider how these rulings apply today, e.g., using a silver spoon vs. a gold dental crown.
The school balances general prohibition with necessity (darurah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الكراهية الفضة وقعت في مجلس أبي جعفر الدوانقي والإمام حاضر وأئمة عصره حاضرون فقالت الأئمة يكره والإمام ساكت فقيل له ما تقول؟ قال : إن وضع فمه في موضع الفضة يكره وإلا فلا. قيل له: من أين لك؟ قال: أرأيت لو كان في أصبعه خاتم فضة فشرب من كفه يكره ذلك فوقفت الكل وتعجب ابن جعفر من جوابه. وفي نوادر هشام في قارورة ذهب أو فضة يصب منها الدهن على رأسه والأشنان أكرهه ولا أكره الغالية وفرق بينهما بأن في الغالية يدخل الإنسان يده فإذا أخرجه إلى الكف لم يكن استعمالاً فأما الدهن فإنه يستعمل ولا يشد الأسنان بالذهب. ولو جدع أنفه لا يتخذ أنفاً من ذهب ويتخذه من الفضة عند الإمام، وعند الثالث يتخذ من الذهب لما روي عن عرفجة أنه أصيب أنفه فاتخذ أنفاً من الفضة فأنتن فأمر النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام بأن يتخذ أنفاً من الذهب، ولأن الفضة والذهب مستويان في الحرمة. وإذا سقطت ثنيته فإنه يكره أن يعيدها ويشدها بذهب أو فضة ولكن يأخذ مذكاة فيجعلها مكانها عند الإمام. وقال أبو يوسف : يشدها بالذهب والفضة في مكانها ؛ كذا في المحيط مع بيان الدليل ا.هـ. وفي العتابية وسلاسل الخيل من الفضة فيها الخلاف المتقدم ا هـ.
Chapter of Dislike. Silver: An incident occurred in the court of Abu Ja'far al-Dawaniqi while the Imam [Abu Hanifa] was present along with the leading scholars of his time. They said it is disliked, and the Imam remained silent. It was said to him: What do you say? He said: If he places his mouth on the place of silver, it is disliked; otherwise not. It was said to him: From where do you derive this? He said: Do you not see that if he had a silver ring on his finger and drank from his palm, that would be disliked. So everyone was amazed, and Ibn Ja'far was astonished at his answer. And in Nawadir Hisham: Regarding a bottle of gold or silver from which oil is poured onto his head and [also] ashes, I dislike it, but I do not dislike ghaliyah [perfume]. And he distinguished between them: in ghaliyah, a person inserts his hand, and when he withdraws it to the palm, it is not use. As for oil, it is used. And teeth are not to be bound with gold. And if his nose is cut off, he should not make a nose of gold; he should make it of silver according to the Imam. According to the third [Muhammad al-Shaybani], he should make it of gold due to the narration from 'Urfajah that his nose was injured, so he made a nose of silver, which putrefied, so the Prophet (peace be upon him) ordered that he make a nose of gold. And because silver and gold are equal in prohibition. And if his front tooth falls out, it is disliked to reattach it and bind it with gold or silver; rather, he should take a slaughtered animal's tooth and put it in its place according to the Imam. Abu Yusuf said: He binds it with gold or silver in its place. Thus in al-Muhit with the explanation of the evidence. And in al-'Utabiyyah: Regarding silver chains of horses, there is the same difference of opinion as previously mentioned.
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage is the commentary on the matn phrase 'الفضة' (silver) from Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It addresses the dislike (karahah) of using gold and silver vessels and prosthetics. The text begins with a famous anecdote: in the court of Caliph Abu Ja'far al-Mansur, the scholars present declared that drinking from a silver vessel is disliked. Imam Abu Hanifa was silent, then gave a nuanced ruling: if one puts his mouth directly on the silver part, it is disliked; otherwise not. He supported this with an analogy: a silver ring on the finger touching the palm while drinking is disliked. The commentator then cites Nawadir Hisham, distinguishing between pouring oil from a gold/silver bottle (disliked) and using perfume (ghaliyah) from such containers (not disliked), because the perfume is applied with the hand and the container is not directly used. Next, the ruling on artificial noses and teeth: if the nose is cut off, Imam Abu Hanifa says to use silver, not gold, while the third imam (Muhammad al-Shaybani) allows gold, citing the hadith of 'Urfajah who made a gold nose after his silver one putrefied. The commentator notes that silver and gold are equal in prohibition (both haram for men to wear as jewelry). For a fallen tooth, Imam Abu Hanifa dislikes reattaching it with gold or silver and recommends using a slaughtered animal's tooth instead. Abu Yusuf, however, permits binding with gold or silver. The text concludes with a cross-reference to silver chains for horses, where the same difference of opinion applies.

Key Terms
الكراهية (al-karāhah): Dislike; a category between permissible and forbidden. The chapter title.
الفضة (al-fiḍḍa): Silver; the substance being discussed regarding vessels and prosthetics.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, what should a person do if his front tooth falls out?
A) Reattach it with gold or silver wire.
B) Leave it missing permanently.
C) Use a tooth from a slaughtered animal as a replacement.
D) Use a gold tooth.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits gold and silver prosthetics due to necessity, but discourages use in vessels.

Ruling on Using a Cloth for Wiping During Wudu and Handkerchiefs
Clarifying that using a cloth for wiping during wudu or a handkerchief is not disliked.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the relied-upon Hanafi ruling on using a cloth for wiping during wudu and explain why the earlier view of kiraha is overruled.

Core Idea
Using a cloth for wiping during wudu is permissible; early view of kiraha is overruled by widespread Muslim practice.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies the ruling on carrying a cloth for wiping during wudu or for mucus. While an early Hanafi source considered it disliked as an innovation, the correct view is that it is not disliked. The basis for this is the established practice of Muslims across generations, supported by the principle 'what the believers see as good is good before Allah.'

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and initial ruling
The act is carrying a cloth (khirqa) for wiping during wudu or for mucus, or a handkerchief (ratm). Some Hanafis considered it makruh as an innovation.
The view of kiraha is from al-Jami' al-Saghir.
2
Locate the proof for kiraha
They cite lack of precedent: the Prophet, Companions, and Followers did not use such cloths; they wiped with their garments.
3
Analyze the rebuttal
The commentator declares the sahih view that it is not disliked, relying on the widespread practice of Muslims ('urf) and the principle 'ما رآه المؤمنون حسناً فهو عند الله حسن' (what the believers see as good is good before Allah).
General practice of Muslims in using napkins and cloths for these purposes establishes permissibility.
4
Determine the operative ruling
The relied-upon ruling is that it is not disliked; it is permissible (mubah).
This is the mufta bihi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (كالخرقة لوضوء أو مخاط والرتم) يعني لا تكره الخرقة لوضوء ولا الرتم. وفي الجامع الصغير : يكره حمل الخرقة التي يمسح بها العرق لأنها بدعة ولم يكن النبي الله يفعل ذلك ولا أحد من الصحابة ولا من التابعين وإنما كانوا يتمسحون بأرديتهم وفيها نوع تجبر، والصحيح أنه لا يكره والرتم لأن عامة المسلمين قد استعملوا في عامة البلدان مناديل للوضوء والخرق لمسح العرق والمخاط ولحمل شيء يحتاج إليه، وما رآه المؤمنون حسناً فهو عند الله حسن، حتى لو حملها لغير
He (the author) said: (Like the cloth for ablution or mucus and the handkerchief) meaning the cloth for ablution is not disliked, nor the handkerchief. In al-Jami' al-Saghir: It is disliked to carry a cloth with which to wipe sweat because it is an innovation; the Prophet did not do that, nor any of the Companions or Followers; they would wipe with their garments, and there is some arrogance. But the correct view is that it is not disliked, and the handkerchief, because the general masses of Muslims have used in most countries napkins for ablution and cloths for wiping sweat and mucus and for carrying something needed, and what the believers see as good is good before Allah, even if he carries it for something else...
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling on carrying a small cloth (khirqa) for wiping during wudu or for dealing with mucus, and also the use of a handkerchief (ratm). The matn states that these are not disliked. The commentator then cites a view from al-Jami' al-Saghir which considers such cloths disliked (makruh) as an innovation, because the Prophet and early Muslims did not use them; they would simply wipe with their garments. However, the commentator declares the sahih (correct) position to be that it is not disliked, basing this on the established practice of Muslims across lands ('amma al-muslimin') who use napkins and cloths for these purposes. This is supported by the principle 'ما رآه المؤمنون حسناً فهو عند الله حسن' (what the believers see as good is good before Allah). The ruling is that it is permissible to use such cloths, and the initial view of kiraha is overridden by the widespread practice. The passage is incomplete but the core ruling is clear: it is not disliked.

Key Terms
الخرقة (al-khirqah): A small piece of cloth used for wiping (e.g., during wudu or for sweat/mucus).
الرتم (al-ratm): A handkerchief or napkin used for wiping or carrying things.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is sunnah.
B) It is makruh because it is an innovation.
C) It is not disliked; it is permissible.
D) It is haram because it wastes water.

Key Takeaway
Using a cloth or handkerchief for wiping during wudu is permissible and not disliked, based on established Muslim practice overruling an earlier view of kiraha.

Prohibition of Gold, Iron, Brass, and Stone Rings
Ruling on rings by material with hadith proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on rings of gold, iron, brass, and stone for men and identify the hadith proofs that establish prohibition.

Core Idea
Gold, iron, brass, and stone rings are haram for men; only silver is permitted with need.

This lesson covers the core ruling on men's rings by material. The Hanafi school prohibits rings made of gold, iron, brass, and stone outright, based on explicit prophetic disapproval. Silver is permitted only if needed, otherwise disliked. We will examine the three key hadiths that form the basis of this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that the act is wearing a ring (takhātum) and that the hukm depends on the material. State the ruling for each material mentioned.
The text clearly states: حرم التختم بالحجر والحديد والصفر والذهب
2
Locate the dalil
Find the three hadiths cited: the Prophet's reaction to a brass ring (smell of idols), an iron ring (adornment of Hellfire), and turning away from a gold ring.
These are ahad hadith, so thubut is zanni, but dalāla is considered qat‘ī.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalālah
Explain how each hadith indicates prohibition. The explicit disapproval and turning away show that these materials are forbidden for men to wear as rings.
The Prophet did not merely dislike but actively turned away, indicating haram.
4
Note the exception for silver
Understand that silver rings are permissible (mubah) for those with a need (e.g., judge, ruler) and otherwise disliked (makruh tanzih). The text explicitly states this.
لا بأس بالتختم بالفضة إذا كان له حاجة إليه كالقاضي والسلطان ولغير ذلك مكروه
5
Identify the mufta bihi
Confirm that the relied-upon Hanafi position is: gold, iron, brass, stone rings are haram; silver is mubah with need, otherwise makruh.
This is consistent across Zahir al-Riwayah and later fatwas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الكراهية
ترك التختم وحرم التختم بالحجر والحديد والصفر والذهب وحل مسمار الذهب يجعل في حجر الفص وشد السن بالفضة وكره إلباس ذهب وحرير صبياً كالخرقة لوضوء أو مخاط والرتم . حق الرجل أن يجعل فص الخاتم في باطن كفه وفي حق المرأة أن تجعله في ظاهر كفها لأنها تزين به دون الرجل ولا بأس بالتختم بالفضة إذا كان له حاجة إليه كالقاضي والسلطان ولغير ذلك مكروه لما روي أنه عليه الصلاة والسلام رأى في يد رجل خاتماً أصفر فقال : مالي أجد منك رائحة الأصنام. ورأى في يد آخر خاتم حديد فقال : ما لي أرى عليك حلية أهل النار. وروي عن ابن عمر أن رجلاً جلس إلى النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وعليه خاتم ذهب فأعرض عنه . والتختم بالذهب حرام ومن الناس من أطلق التختم بحجر يقال له يشب لأنه ليس بحجر إذ ليس له ثقل الحجر والحلقة هي معتبرة لأن قوام الخاتم بها ولا يعتبر بالفص لأنه يجوز من الحجر والأولى أن لا يتختم إذا كان لا يحتاج إليه ولا بأس بمسمار الذهب يجعل في حجر الفص يعني في ثقبه لأنه تابع كالعلم فلا يعد لابساً ولا يزيد وزنه على مثقال لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام اتخذه من ورق ولا تزده على مثقال ورد النص بجواز التختم بالعقيق وقال عليه الصلاة والسلام تختموا بالعقيق فإنه مبارك الحديث. وفي الحاوي : ولا بأس أن يتخذ الرجل خاتم فضة، فإن جعل فصه من عقيق أو ياقوت أو فيروزج أو زمرد فلا بأس به، وإن نقش عليه اسمه أو اسم أبيه أو اسماً من أسماء الله فلا بأس به، ولا ينبغي أن ينقش عليه تماثيل من طير أو هوام الأرض ولا بأس بأن يشرب من كفه وفي خنصره خاتم ذهب، ولا بأس بمسمار الذهب يجعل في الفضة وفي الينابيع : كان يتختم باليمين وأبو بكر وعمر بالشمال وفي الفتاوى : وينبغي أن يلبس الخاتم في خنصره اليسرى دون سائر أصابعه ولا ينبغي أن يخضب يد الصغير أو رجله. قال رحمه الله: (والأفضل لغير السلطان والقاضي ترك التختم وحرم التختم بالحجر والحديد والصفر والذهب وحل مسمار الذهب يجعل في حجر الفص) وقد تقدم بيانه
The Book of Disliked Acts. Abandoning wearing a ring, and wearing a ring of stone, iron, brass, and gold is prohibited, and the nail of gold placed in the stone of the bezel is permissible, and mending a tooth with silver, and it is disliked to dress a boy in gold and silk, like the cloth for ablution or mucus and the thread. The right of a man is to place the bezel of the ring on the inside of his palm, and for a woman, on the back of her hand because she adorns with it unlike the man. There is no harm in wearing a silver ring if he has a need for it, like the judge and the ruler, and for other than that it is disliked, due to what was narrated that he (peace be upon him) saw a yellow ring on the hand of a man and said: 'Why do I find from you the smell of idols?' And he saw an iron ring on the hand of another and said: 'Why do I see on you the adornment of the People of Hellfire?' And it is narrated from Ibn Umar that a man sat with the Prophet while he had a gold ring, so he turned away from him. Wearing a gold ring is prohibited. And some people permitted wearing a ring of a stone called yashb because it is not a stone as it has no weight of stone. The ring (circlet) is what is considered because the structure of the ring is by it, and the bezel is not considered because it is permissible from stone. It is better not to wear a ring if he does not need it. There is no harm in a gold nail placed in the stone of the bezel, meaning in its perforation, because it is a follower like a mark, so he is not considered a wearer, and its weight should not exceed a mithqal due to his saying: 'Take it of silver and do not increase it over a mithqal.' The text came permitting wearing a ring of cornelian, and he said: 'Wear rings of cornelian, for it is blessed' — the hadith. In al-Hawi: There is no harm for a man to take a silver ring, and if he makes its bezel of cornelian, sapphire, turquoise, or emerald, there is no harm. If he engraves on it his name or his father's name or a name of Allah, there is no harm. It is not appropriate to engrave images of birds or crawling creatures. There is no harm in drinking from his palm while having a gold ring on his little finger. There is no harm in a gold nail placed in silver. And in al-Yanabi: He used to wear the ring on the right hand, and Abu Bakr and Umar on the left. And in al-Fatawa: It is appropriate to wear the ring on the left little finger, not on other fingers. It is not appropriate to dye the hand or foot of a small child. He said: 'The better for other than the ruler and judge is to abandon wearing a ring, and wearing a ring of stone, iron, brass, and gold is prohibited, and the gold nail placed in the stone of the bezel is permissible.' And its explanation has preceded.
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage from the Book of Disliked Acts opens the chapter with a summary of rulings on wearing rings. The author (Ibn Nujaym) begins by stating the matn: it is better for non-rulers to abandon wearing rings altogether, and it is haram to wear rings of stone, iron, brass, or gold. However, a gold nail in the bezel is permissible as it is a mere follower. The sharh then elaborates: a man should place the bezel inward, while woman outward for adornment. Silver rings are permissible for those who need them (like judges and rulers) but makruh for others, supported by three hadiths: the Prophet disapproved of a yellow ring (comparing it to idols), an iron ring (adornment of Hellfire), and turned away from a gold ring. Hence gold is haram. There is a nuance about stone: some allow yashb stone because it lacks weight. The ring's metal (circlet) is what matters, not the stone. A gold nail in the stone is allowed if less than a mithqal, citing a hadith. Cornelian (aqiq) is specifically recommended. The passage also quotes al-Hawi and al-Yanabi on proper placement and prohibition of engraving images. The bottom line: for Hanafis, wearing rings of gold, iron, brass, and most stones is haram for men; silver is either mubah (with need) or makruh (without).

Key Terms
التختم (al-takhātum): The act of wearing a ring (khātam) on the finger.
الفص (al-faṣṣ): The stone or gem set in the ring; the bezel.
الحلقة (al-ḥalqah): The metal band or circlet of the ring; its 'quwām' (structure).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling on a man wearing an iron ring without any specific need?
A) Mustahab (recommended)
B) Mubah (permissible)
C) Makruh (disliked)
D) Haram (prohibited)

Key Takeaway
Gold, iron, brass, and stone rings are haram for men; silver rings are only permissible with a legitimate need, otherwise makruh.

intermediate
8 min
The Circlet vs. the Stone: Gold Nails and Gemstones
Detailed ruling on ring components and gold nail

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi distinction between the ring's circlet and its stone, and apply the rulings on gold nails in the bezel and recommended gemstones like cornelian.

Core Idea
The metal circlet determines the ring's ruling; gold nails in the stone are permissible as followers, and cornelian is recommended.

This lesson delves into a nuance: the ring is defined by its metal band (halqah), not the stone (fas). This principle explains why a gold nail in the stone is allowed, and why stones like cornelian are recommended. We also examine the condition that the gold nail must not exceed one mithqal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two components of a ring
Understand that a ring consists of a metal circlet (halqah) and a stone/gem (fas). The text states that the circlet is what matters (قوام الخاتم بها).
الحلقة هي معتبرة لأن قوام الخاتم بها
2
Apply the principle to gold nails
A gold nail placed in the stone (to fix it) is considered a follower (tabi‘) and not the main ring. Therefore, it is permissible as long as it weighs less than a mithqal.
ولا بأس بمسمار الذهب يجعل في حجر الفص لأنه تابع كالعلم
3
Learn the mithqal condition
The gold nail must not exceed one mithqal (approx. 4.25 grams), based on the hadith: 'اتخذه من ورق ولا تزده على مثقال'.
رخصة للرجل أن يتخذ خاتم فضة ولا يزيد على مثقال
4
Note the recommendation of cornelian
The text reports a hadith: 'تختموا بالعقيق فإنه مبارك' (Wear rings of cornelian, for it is blessed). Cornelian is a recommended stone.
رد النص بجواز التختم بالعقيق
5
Distinguish yashb stone
Some permitted yashb (jade) because it is not a 'stone' (lacks weight). This shows the rule is about the circlet, not the stone material.
من الناس من أطلق التختم بحجر يقال له يشب لأنه ليس بحجر

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms
مسمار الذهب (mismār al-dhahab): A gold nail used to fix a stone in a ring's bezel.
مثقال (mithqāl): A unit of weight approximately 4.25 grams, used in the hadith to limit gold.
العقيق (al-‘aqīq): Cornelian, a red gemstone recommended by the Prophet.

What is the maximum weight of a gold nail in a ring's stone according to the Hanafi school?
A) Half a mithqal
B) One mithqal
C) Two mithqals
D) No limit

Etiquette of Wearing Rings: Hand, Finger, and Bezel Placement
Etiquette and placement rules for rings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the recommended placement of a ring for men and women and identify prohibited engravings according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Men wear the bezel inward, women outward; the sunnah is to wear the ring on the left little finger; engravings of living beings are prohibited.

Beyond the material rulings, the Hanafi school also prescribes proper etiquette for wearing rings. This lesson covers the placement of the bezel (inward for men, outward for women), the preferred finger (left little finger), the permissibility of wearing on the right hand, and the prohibition of engraving animate images. These adab are drawn from the practice of the Prophet and the Companions.

Guided Path
1
Bezel placement for men vs women
The text states: 'حق الرجل أن يجعل فص الخاتم في باطن كفه وفي حق المرأة أن تجعله في ظاهر كفها'. Understand the reasoning: women wear it outward for adornment, men inward.
Reason: المرأة تزين به
2
Preferred finger and hand
From al-Fatawa: 'وينبغي أن يلبس الخاتم في خنصره اليسرى'. The left little finger is recommended. Some reports show the Prophet wearing it on the right, and Abu Bakr and Umar on the left.
اليمين vs الشمال: خلاف
3
Prohibition of engraving images
The text says: 'ولا ينبغي أن ينقش عليه تماثيل من طير أو هوام الأرض'. Engraving animate creatures is not appropriate.
Includes birds and crawling creatures.
4
Permissible engravings
Engraving one's name, father's name, or a name of Allah is permissible.
From al-Hawi.
5
Dyeing hands/feet of children
The text ends with: 'ولا ينبغي أن يخضب يد الصغير أو رجله'. It is not appropriate to dye the hands or feet of a small child.
This is also part of adab, unrelated to rings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

If he engraves on it his name or his father's name or a name of Allah, there is no harm. It is not appropriate to engrave images of birds or crawling creatures. There is no harm in drinking from his palm while having a gold ring on his little finger. There is no harm in a gold nail placed in silver. And in al-Yanabi: He used to wear the ring on the right hand, and Abu Bakr and Umar on the left. And in al-Fatawa: It is appropriate to wear the ring on the left little finger, not on other fingers. It is not appropriate to dye the hand or foot of a small child. He said: 'The better for other than the ruler and judge is to abandon wearing a ring, and wearing a ring of stone, iron, brass, and gold is prohibited, and the gold nail placed in the stone of the bezel is permissible.' And its explanation has preceded.
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage from the Book of Disliked Acts opens the chapter with a summary of rulings on wearing rings. The author (Ibn Nujaym) begins by stating the matn: it is better for non-rulers to abandon wearing rings altogether, and it is haram to wear rings of stone, iron, brass, or gold. However, a gold nail in the bezel is permissible as it is a mere follower. The sharh then elaborates: a man should place the bezel inward, while woman outward for adornment. Silver rings are permissible for those who need them (like judges and rulers) but makruh for others, supported by three hadiths: the Prophet disapproved of a yellow ring (comparing it to idols), an iron ring (adornment of Hellfire), and turned away from a gold ring. Hence gold is haram. There is a nuance about stone: some allow yashb stone because it lacks weight. The ring's metal (circlet) is what matters, not the stone. A gold nail in the stone is allowed if less than a mithqal, citing a hadith. Cornelian (aqiq) is specifically recommended. The passage also quotes al-Hawi and al-Yanabi on proper placement and prohibition of engraving images. The bottom line: for Hanafis, wearing rings of gold, iron, brass, and most stones is haram for men; silver is either mubah (with need) or makruh (without).

Key Terms
خنصر (khinṣir): The little finger (pinky).
باطن الكف (bāṭin al-kaff): The inside/palm side of the hand.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following engravings is prohibited on a ring according to the Hanafi school?
A) A name of Allah
B) A man's own name
C) A bird
D) A name of the Prophet

Key Takeaway
Men place the bezel inward, women outward; the preferred finger is the left little finger; engraving animate images is prohibited.

Mending a Tooth: Silver Permissible, Gold Disputed
Intra-Hanafi dispute on mending a tooth with gold.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on mending a tooth with silver and gold, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Core Idea
Silver is permissible for mending a tooth; gold is haram for men according to Abu Hanifa, but Muhammad permits it by analogy to a gold nail in a ring.

When a tooth becomes loose, it may be stabilized with a wire. The Hanafi school permits silver for this purpose, but gold is a point of contention between the two great imams. In this lesson, we examine the ruling, the proof for each position, and the relied-upon view.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling on silver
Identify that the text explicitly permits mending a tooth with silver. This is unanimous.
Silver is halal because it serves a medical need without inherent prohibition.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position on gold
According to Abu Hanifa, gold is not permissible for mending a tooth. This is the well-known (zahir al-riwayah) position.
Gold is haram for men as adornment.
3
Identify Muhammad's opposing view
Muhammad al-Shaybani permits gold, analogizing it to a gold nail in a ring bezel which is permissible.
This is a valid ikhtilaf within the madhhab.
4
Follow the opposing proof
Muhammad's proof is qiyas: just as a gold nail in a ring bezel is allowed (as a follower), gold wire for a tooth is allowed.
Both involve gold as a secondary, functional element.
5
Analyze the Hanafi response
The response distinguishes: the nail is a mere follower (tabi') and not primary adornment, while the tooth wire is worn directly and thus prohibited.
The distinction hinges on the concept of tabi' versus primary use.
6
Determine the thamarah and mufta bihi
The practical difference is whether using gold incurs sin. The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's prohibition.
The author indicates tarjih by saying 'we have explained it earlier.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : وشد السن بالفضة) يعني يحل شد السن المتحرك بالفضة ولا يحل بالذهب، وقال محمد يحل بالذهب أيضاً وقدمنا بيان ذلك .
He said (may Allah have mercy on him): 'And mending a tooth with silver.' Meaning, it is permissible to tie a loose tooth with silver, and not permissible with gold. And Muhammad said: it is also permissible with gold. And we have presented its explanation before.
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This is a short intra-Hanafi dispute within the commentary on the matn. The author (Ibn Nujaym) reports the ruling: mending a loose tooth with silver (to keep it in place) is halal, but with gold it is not halal according to Abu Hanifa. Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, however, permits gold as well. The text notes that the explanation has already been given (presumably earlier in the book). The relied-upon position is that of Abu Hanifa (gold not permissible) because gold is inherently haram for men to use, except in exceptional circumstances. Silver is permissible because it serves a medical need without involving prohibition.

Key Terms
شد السن (shadd al-sinn): Tying or mending a loose tooth with a wire or other material to stabilize it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani regarding mending a tooth with gold?
A) It is prohibited
B) It is permissible
C) It is disliked
D) It is obligatory

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, mending a tooth with gold is prohibited (mufta bihi) despite Muhammad's permission, due to the general prohibition of gold for men.

Dressing Boys in Gold and Silk
Ruling with analogical proof from wine

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on dressing a male child in gold or silk and apply the analogy (qiyas) from wine to derive the ruling.

Core Idea
Enabling a prohibited act is itself prohibited.

This brief passage from Kanz al-Daqa’iq addresses a subtle but important extension of the prohibition of silk and gold for men. While the matn uses the term 'makruh', the commentator explains that this is makruh tahrimi, equivalent to haram. The proof is a clear analogy to wine: just as giving wine to a child is haram, so is dressing him in silk or gold.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling in the matn
The matn states 'it is disliked (kariha) to dress a boy in gold and silk'. Note the term 'makruh' – in Hanafi terminology this could be either tanzihi or tahrimi. The commentator will clarify.
The matn alone does not fully convey the weight of the prohibition.
2
Understand the underlying prohibition
The sharh explains that the wearing of gold and silk is haram for adult males. This is the established ruling (the asl). Without this, the analogy would not hold.
The prohibition of wearing for men is agreed upon across all schools.
3
Apply the analogy (qiyas)
The text compares dressing a boy in gold/silk to giving wine to a child. The illa (cause) is that enabling or causing a prohibited act is itself prohibited. Since drinking wine is haram, giving it is haram. Similarly, since wearing gold/silk is haram, dressing someone in them is haram.
This is a clear qiyas jali from the original ruling.
4
Derive the final ruling
Thus the ruling on dressing a boy is haram (or makruh tahrimi). The term 'makruh' in the matn is interpreted as makruh tahrimi, which is effectively haram in terms of sin.
Some may mistakenly think it is merely disliked (makruh tanzih), but the sharh corrects this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (وكره إلباس ذهب وحرير صبياً لأن التحريم لما ثبت في حق الذكور حرم اللبس حرم الإلباس كالخمر لما حرم شربها حرم سقيها للصبي.
He said: 'And it is disliked to dress a boy in gold and silk because when prohibition is established for males, wearing is prohibited, and dressing is also prohibited, like wine: when its drinking is prohibited, giving it to a boy to drink is also prohibited.'
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This ruling addresses dressing young boys (who are not yet legally responsible) in gold and silk. The matn says it is 'makruh' (disliked), but the sharh explains the underlying reason: since wearing gold and silk is haram for adult males, dressing a boy in them is also haram—because enabling a prohibited act is itself prohibited. The analogy to wine is clear: just as giving wine to a child who cannot drink (or any person) is haram because drinking is haram, similarly dressing a child in gold or silk is haram because wearing is haram. The term 'makruh' in the matn is understood as makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked), which is equivalent to haram in terms of sin. Thus, the correct Hanafi position is that it is haram to dress a male child in gold or silk, even if the child himself is not sinful.

Key Terms
إلباس (ilbās): The act of dressing someone else; clothing another person.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is dressing a male child in gold or silk prohibited?
A) Because the child will become arrogant.
B) Because it is a waste of money.
C) Because wearing gold/silk is haram for men, and enabling that act is also haram.
D) Because it is against the sunnah to dress children in expensive clothes.

Key Takeaway
Enabling a prohibited act, such as dressing a boy in gold or silk, is itself prohibited by analogy to giving wine.

Ruling on Using a Cloth for Ablution and Wiping
Ruling on cloth for wiping and its intra-Hanafi difference

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the correct Hanafi ruling on carrying a cloth for wiping sweat/mucus and identify the condition that changes its permissibility.

Core Idea
A cloth for wiping is permissible due to custom, but disliked without need.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, the author discusses the ruling on using a cloth for ablution or wiping sweat. The matn mentions it among items that are not disliked. However, a report in al-Jami' al-Saghir considers it a disliked innovation. The sharh resolves this by citing the widespread practice of Muslims and the principle that 'what the believers consider good is good with Allah.' The correct Hanafi position is that it is not disliked when there is a legitimate need, but disliked when carried without need.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act
Determine the act under discussion: carrying a cloth for wiping sweat or mucus, or using one for ablution.
The cloth is called al-khirqah.
2
Note the two views
One view (in al-Jami' al-Saghir) considers it disliked because it was not the practice of the Salaf. The correct view, adopted by the author, is that it is not disliked.
The dispute is intra-Hanafi.
3
Understand the basis of the correct view
The correct view relies on the fact that Muslims in all lands have commonly used handkerchiefs and cloths for necessity, establishing an 'urf (custom). The maxim 'What the believers consider good is good with Allah' supports this.
Custom can transform a mere practice into a permissible one.
4
Apply the condition
If the cloth is carried for a need (e.g., wiping after ablution or removing mucus), it is mubah. If carried without any need, it is makruh.
Need makes the difference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (كالخرقة لوضوء أو مخاط والرتم) يعني لا تكره الخرقة لوضوء ولا الرتم. وفي الجامع الصغير : يكره حمل الخرقة التي يمسح بها العرق لأنها بدعة ولم يكن النبي الله يفعل ذلك ولا أحد من الصحابة ولا من التابعين وإنما كانوا يتمسحون بأرديتهم وفيها نوع تجبر، والصحيح أنه لا يكره والرتم لأن عامة المسلمين قد استعملوا في عامة البلدان مناديل للوضوء والخرق لمسح العرق والمخاط ولحمل شيء يحتاج إليه، وما رآه المؤمنون حسناً فهو عند الله حسن، حتى لو حملها لغير حاجة يكره. والرتم هو الرتيمة وهي الخيط للتذكر ليعقد في الأصابع وكذا الرتمة فقيل الرتم ضرب من الشجر. وقال : معناه كان الرجل إذا خرج إلى سفر عمد إلى هذه الشجرة فعقد بعض أغصانها ببعض فإذا وأصابه بتلك الحالة قال لم تخن امرأتي، وإن أصابه قد انحل قال خانتني. ثم الرتيمة قد تشبه بالتميمة على بعض الناس وهو خيط كان يربط في العنق أو في اليد في الجاهلية لدفع المضرة عن أنفسهم. وذكر في حدود الإيمان أنه كفر. والرتيمة مباح لأنها تربط للتذكير عند اليد في الجاهلية لدفع المضرة عن أنفسهم. وذكر في حدود الإيمان أنه كفر . والرتيمة مباح لأنها تربط للتذكير عند النسيان وقد ورد أنه عليه الصلاة والسلام أمر بعض أصحابه بها وتعلق غرض صحيح فلا يكره بخلاف التميمة فإنه عليه الصلاة والسلام قال فيها أن الرقي والتمائم والنودة شركة على ما يجيء إن شاء الله تعالى ..
He said: 'Like the cloth for ablution or mucus and the thread.' Meaning, the cloth for ablution is not disliked, nor the thread. And in al-Jami' al-Saghir: It is disliked to carry the cloth with which one wipes sweat because it is an innovation (bid'ah) and the Prophet did not do that, nor any of the Companions or the Successors; rather they would wipe with their cloaks, and that involves a kind of arrogance. But the correct view is that it is not disliked, nor is the thread, because the general masses of Muslims have used in most lands handkerchiefs for ablution and cloths for wiping sweat and mucus and for carrying something needed. And what the believers consider good is good before Allah. But if one carries it without need, it is disliked. The word 'r-t-m' refers to the 'rutaymah', which is a thread tied on the fingers for remembrance; also 'ratmah'. It is said that 'r-t-m' is a type of tree. And it is said: the meaning is that when a man went out on a journey, he would go to this tree and tie some of its branches together; if he returned and found them in that state, he would say 'my wife has not been unfaithful', and if he found them untied, he would say 'she has been unfaithful'. Then the 'rutaymah' resembles the 'tamimah' (amulet) for some people; it is a thread that was tied around the neck or hand in the Jahiliyyah to ward off harm. It is mentioned in 'Hudud al-Iman' that it is kufr (disbelief). But the 'rutaymah' is permissible because it is tied to remind in case of forgetfulness, and it has been reported that the Prophet (peace be upon him) ordered some of his Companions to do it, and there is a correct purpose, so it is not disliked, unlike the 'tamimah' about which the Prophet said: 'Ruqyah, tamimah, and tiwalah are shirk' as will come, Allah willing.
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage discusses three items mentioned in the matn: (1) using a cloth for ablution (khirqah li-l-wudu'), (2) for wiping mucus or sweat, and (3) the 'r-t-m' (thread for remembrance). The sharh clarifies: The cloth for ablution is not disliked; it is permissible. Regarding the cloth for wiping sweat, al-Jami' al-Saghir reports it as disliked because it was not the practice of the Salaf, but the correct view (al-sahih) is that it is not disliked because Muslims have generally used them for necessities. However, carrying it without need is disliked. As for the 'r-t-m' (also called rutaymah), it is a thread tied around the finger to remember something. Some pre-Islamic practice involved tying tree branches for suspicion of wife's fidelity—that resembles tamimah (amulet) which is shirk. But the rutaymah for remembrance (like a string tied to avoid forgetting) is permissible because the Prophet himself ordered some Companions to use it for a valid purpose. It is not disliked. This shows the Hanafi principle that customs of Muslims ('urf) can render acts permissible if they serve a legitimate need and are not associated with shirk.

Key Terms
الخرقة (al-khirqah): A piece of cloth used for wiping or as a handkerchief for ablution.
البدعة (al-bid'ah): An innovation in religion, which could be disliked or haram.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the correct Hanafi view in this passage, when is carrying a cloth for wiping disliked?
A) Always, because it is an innovation.
B) When it is carried without any need.
C) When it is used for ablution.
D) When it is made of expensive material.

Key Takeaway
Carrying a cloth for wiping is permissible if needed, but disliked without need.

Ruling on Tying a Thread for Remembrance (Ratam/Rutaymah)
Ruling on the remembrance thread and distinction from amulet

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the permissible rutaymah (remembrance thread) and the prohibited tamimah (amulet), and state the ruling for each.

Core Idea
Tying a thread to remember something is permissible; tying it as an amulet for protection is shirk.

The passage also discusses the word 'r-t-m' (الرتم), interpreted as 'rutaymah' — a thread tied on the finger to aid memory. The author clarifies that some pre-Islamic Arabs used a similar practice for superstitious reasons (tying tree branches to test fidelity). However, the Prophet (saw) himself instructed some Companions to use a thread for remembrance, establishing its permissibility. On the other hand, the 'tamimah' (an amulet worn for protection) is explicitly condemned as shirk. This lesson explains the critical distinction based on intention and purpose.

Guided Path
1
Identify the object and its name
The Arabic word 'r-t-m' (الرتم) refers to a thread tied on the fingers, also called 'rutaymah' (الرتيمة). It is used for remembrance.
Do not confuse with 'ratam' which can also mean a type of tree.
2
Distinguish the superstitious pre-Islamic practice
Some pre-Islamic Arabs would tie branches of a tree before a journey to test wives' fidelity. That practice is not relevant to the thread for remembrance.
This background helps avoid conflation.
3
Note the prophetic approval
The Prophet (saw) ordered some Companions to use a thread for remembrance, indicating its permissibility.
This is a strong proof (dalil).
4
Contrast with tamimah
Tamimah is an amulet worn for protection, which is shirk. The Prophet said: 'Ruqyah, tamimah, and tiwalah are shirk.'
The key difference is intention and belief.
5
Determine the ruling
Rutaymah (remembrance thread) is mubah (permissible). Tamimah (protective amulet) is haram and even kufr if believed to have independent power.
The ruling hinges on purpose and association with shirk.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (كالخرقة لوضوء أو مخاط والرتم) يعني لا تكره الخرقة لوضوء ولا الرتم. وفي الجامع الصغير : يكره حمل الخرقة التي يمسح بها العرق لأنها بدعة ولم يكن النبي الله يفعل ذلك ولا أحد من الصحابة ولا من التابعين وإنما كانوا يتمسحون بأرديتهم وفيها نوع تجبر، والصحيح أنه لا يكره والرتم لأن عامة المسلمين قد استعملوا في عامة البلدان مناديل للوضوء والخرق لمسح العرق والمخاط ولحمل شيء يحتاج إليه، وما رآه المؤمنون حسناً فهو عند الله حسن، حتى لو حملها لغير حاجة يكره. والرتم هو الرتيمة وهي الخيط للتذكر ليعقد في الأصابع وكذا الرتمة فقيل الرتم ضرب من الشجر. وقال : معناه كان الرجل إذا خرج إلى سفر عمد إلى هذه الشجرة فعقد بعض أغصانها ببعض فإذا وأصابه بتلك الحالة قال لم تخن امرأتي، وإن أصابه قد انحل قال خانتني. ثم الرتيمة قد تشبه بالتميمة على بعض الناس وهو خيط كان يربط في العنق أو في اليد في الجاهلية لدفع المضرة عن أنفسهم. وذكر في حدود الإيمان أنه كفر. والرتيمة مباح لأنها تربط للتذكير عند اليد في الجاهلية لدفع المضرة عن أنفسهم. وذكر في حدود الإيمان أنه كفر . والرتيمة مباح لأنها تربط للتذكير عند النسيان وقد ورد أنه عليه الصلاة والسلام أمر بعض أصحابه بها وتعلق غرض صحيح فلا يكره بخلاف التميمة فإنه عليه الصلاة والسلام قال فيها أن الرقي والتمائم والنودة شركة على ما يجيء إن شاء الله تعالى ..
He said: 'Like the cloth for ablution or mucus and the thread.' Meaning, the cloth for ablution is not disliked, nor the thread. Key Terms
الرتم / الرتيمة (al-ratam / al-rutaymah): A thread tied on the finger as a reminder; also a tree or a branch-tying practice.
التميمة (al-tamīmah): An amulet worn for protection, considered shirk in Islam.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What evidence does the author give to prove that the rutaymah (remembrance thread) is permissible?
A) The Companions always used it.
B) The Prophet ordered some Companions to use it.
C) It is mentioned in the Quran.
D) It was a practice of the Salaf.

Key Takeaway
Tying a thread for remembrance is permissible; wearing an amulet for protection is shirk.

The 'Awrah of Men: Definition and Gradation
Ruling statement defining 'awrah and its graded legal consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of a man's 'awrah and explain the gradation of penalty for uncovering different parts.

Core Idea
A man's 'awrah is from navel to knee, but navel is excluded and knee included, with graded consequences for exposure.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq defines the 'awrah of a man according to the Hanafi school. It is not enough to know the boundaries; one must also understand that the severity of punishment varies: the knee is less severe, the thigh more severe, and the navel most severe. This reflects the legal principle that not all parts of the 'awrah are equal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the boundaries
Note the exact limits: between the navel and knee, with navel excluded and knee included.
The navel is not 'awrah; the knee is 'awrah.
2
Understand the gradation
The text ranks exposure: knee (gentle rebuke), thigh (harsh rebuke), navel (beating). This shows a sliding scale of punishment.
The severity increases as the area is more sensitive.
3
Note the proof reference
The author refers to the Book of Wudu for the proof of this definition.
The proof is established elsewhere; this passage assumes knowledge of it.
4
Apply the ruling in practice
In daily life, a man must cover at least from navel to knee. Different consequences apply if he fails.
This affects changing clothes, swimming, etc.
5
Recognize the legal implication for ta'zir
The gradation informs the discretionary punishment (ta'zir) for exposing 'awrah.
The judge may tailor the punishment according to the part exposed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (وينظر الرجل إلى الرجل إلا العورة وهي ما بين السرة والركبة، والسرة ليست من العورة والركبة منها ، وإنما لم ينبه المؤلف هنا لما قدم في كتاب الوضوء وقد بينا الدليل هناك . وحكم العورة في الركبة أخف منه في الفخذ، وفي الفخذ أخف منه في السرة حتى ينكر عليه في كشف الركبة برفق وفي الفخذ بعنف وفي السرة بضرب. وفي التتمة والإبانة : كان أبو حنيفة لا يرى بأساً بنظر الحمامي إلى عورة الرجل، وفي الكافي : وعظم الساق ليس بعورة. وفي الذخيرة وما جاز النظر إليه جاز مسه قال محمد بن مقاتل : لا بأس أن يتولى صاحب الحمام عورة إنسان بيده عند التنور إذا كان يغض بصره. قال الفقيه : وهذه في حال الضرورة لا في غيرها، وينبغي لكل إنسان أن يتولى عورته بنفسه عند التنور. وفي التتمة : البيت الصغير في الحمام يدخله الرجل يحلق عانته هل يحل له أن يكون فيه عرياناً حتى يعصر إزاره؟ فقال في المدة اليسيرة يجوز. وقال أبو الفضل : لا بأس به. وقال غيره: يأثم به
He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: 'And a man looks at a man except the 'awrah, which is between the navel and the knee, and the navel is not part of the 'awrah, and the knee is part of it.' And the author did not mention it here because he already presented it in the Book of Wudu, and we have explained the proof there. And the ruling of 'awrah regarding the knee is lighter than regarding the thigh, and regarding the thigh is lighter than regarding the navel, such that one is rebuked gently for uncovering the knee, harshly for the thigh, and with beating for the navel. In al-Tatimma and al-Ibana: Abu Hanifa did not see any harm in the bath attendant looking at a man's 'awrah. In al-Kafi: The shin bone is not 'awrah. In al-Dhakhira: Whatever is permissible to look at is permissible to touch. Muhammad ibn Muqatil said: There is no harm if the bathhouse owner handles a person's 'awrah with his hand at the furnace if he lowers his gaze. Al-Faqih said: This is only in a state of necessity, not otherwise, and every person should handle his own 'awrah at the furnace. In al-Tatimma: A small room in the bathhouse that a man enters to shave his pubic hair—is it permissible for him to be naked in it until he wrings his waist wrapper? He said: For a short time, it is permissible. Abu al-Fadl said: There is no harm in it. Others said: He sins by it.
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage defines the 'awrah of a man according to the Hanafi school: from the navel to the knee, with the navel excluded and the knee included. The proof is referenced from the Book of Wudu. Interestingly, the ruling has gradations: uncovering the knee is less severe than the thigh, and the navel is the most severe. For hamam (bathhouse) attendants, Abu Hanifa originally allowed looking at the 'awrah due to necessity, but later scholars restricted it. The shin is not 'awrah, so it may be exposed. The principle that what is permissible to look at is permissible to touch is mentioned, but with caution. For handling the 'awrah in the bath (e.g., for washing), it is only permitted in necessity, and one should ideally do it oneself. The passage ends with a difference of opinion on being naked in the bath to wring out a wet garment: some permit it for a short time, others consider it sinful. This shows the detailed application of 'awrah rulings in daily life.

Key Terms
عورة (awrah): Parts of the body that must be covered; for men, from the navel to the knee inclusive of the knee but not the navel.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, which part of the body is NOT included in the 'awrah of a man?
A) The knee.
B) The thigh.
C) The navel.
D) The area between navel and knee.

Key Takeaway
A man's 'awrah is from navel to knee, but navel is excluded and knee included, with graded punishment for exposure.

Rulings of the Hamam: Looking, Touching, and Necessity
Ruling statement on hamam-specific applications of 'awrah rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on looking at and touching the 'awrah in the context of a bathhouse, and identify the conditions under which it is permissible.

Core Idea
In the hamam, necessity can permit looking at and even touching the 'awrah, but the original ruling is prohibition.

This passage addresses practical scenarios in the public bathhouse (hamam). Abu Hanifa originally allowed bathhouse attendants to look at the 'awrah due to custom, but later scholars restricted it to necessity. Moreover, the shin is not 'awrah, and touching follows the same rule as looking. The passage ends with a difference of opinion on being naked in the bath to wring out a wet garment — a reminder that even in necessity, the minimum exposure is required.

Guided Path
1
Understand the original allowance for hamam workers
Abu Hanifa initially allowed hamam attendants to look at men's 'awrah due to the widespread custom and necessity of the work.
Later Hanafi scholars restricted this to genuine necessity (e.g., lack of other attendants).
2
Identify the condition of necessity
Al-Faqih clarifies that the permissibility is only in a state of necessity (ḍarūrah), not otherwise.
The default ruling is prohibition; necessity makes it permissible.
3
Note the ruling on the shin
The shin is not 'awrah, so it may be exposed without problem.
This clarifies a common point of confusion.
4
Apply the principle: looking and touching
Whatever is permissible to look at is permissible to touch (e.g., the shin). However, for the 'awrah, touching is only allowed in necessity and with lowered gaze.
Muhammad ibn Muqatil allows the owner to handle the 'awrah at the furnace if he lowers his gaze.
5
Analyze the disagreement on being naked to wring the garment
Some scholars permit being naked for a short time to wring a wet waist wrapper, while others consider it sinful.
This shows even within necessity, there is a spectrum of opinion.

Medical Necessity and Looking at a Woman's Wound
Ruling on a doctor looking at a woman's wound when no female doctor is available

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a male doctor may look at a woman's wound and apply the maxim 'necessity is measured by its extent'.

Core Idea
Necessity permits the prohibited, but only to the minimum required extent.

In the Hanafi school, looking at a non-mahram woman's private parts is generally forbidden. But what if a woman has a wound and no female doctor is available? This lesson unpacks the ruling from the classical text, showing how necessity creates a limited exception. You will also learn the related principle that governs such exceptions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
First, determine what act is being discussed (looking at a woman's body) and its default ruling in Hanafi fiqh (haram).
The text uses the principle 'looking at the same gender is lighter' to establish the default.
2
Recognize the exception for necessity
Next, identify the condition that creates an exception: darurah (necessity) when no female doctor is available. The ruling changes from haram to permissible (mubah).
The text explicitly states the doctor should first try to teach a woman to treat the patient.
3
List the conditions for the exception
The exception is not unrestricted. The text gives conditions: cover all parts except the wound, look only at the wound, lower the gaze, and limit the exposure to the minimum.
These conditions operationalize the principle 'necessity is measured by its extent'.
4
Apply the principle of darurah
Finally, understand the legal maxim that governs such exceptions: 'ma thabata lil-darurati yataqaddaru bi-qadariha' (what is established for necessity is limited to its extent). This ensures that the permission does not become a license for excess.
The same principle applies to other necessity cases like eating carrion or speaking falsehood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومس وجدت فلا يجوز له أن ينظر لأن نظر الجنس إلى الجنس أخف. وينبغي للطبيب أن يعلم امرأة إن أمكن وإن لم يكن ستر كل عضو منها سوى موضع الوجع ثم ينظر ويغض ببصره عن غير ذلك الموضع إن استطاع لأن ما ثبت للضرورة يتقدر بقدرها، وإذا أراد أن يتزوج امرأة فلا بأس أن ينظر إليها وإن خاف أن يشتهي لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام «انظر إليها لأنه أحرى أن يدوم بينكما ولا يجوز له أن يمس وجهها ولا كفها وإن أمن الشهوة لوجود المحرم ولانعدام الضرورة وقال عليه الصلاة والسلام من مس كف امرأة ليس له فيها سبيل وضع على كفه جمر يوم القيامة» قال في التتارخانية : أصاب امرأة قرحة في موضع لا يحل للرجل النظر إليه، لم يوجد امرأة تداويها ولم يقدر أن يعلم امرأة تداويها يستر منها كل شيء إلا موضع القرحة ويغض بصره ما أمكن ويداويها. وفي المحيط أيضاً ويجوز للمرأة إذا كانت تولد أخرى أن تنظر إلى فرجها وأن تمس فرجها .اهـ.
And if he touches? Found, then it is not permissible for him to look, because looking at the same gender is lighter. It is recommended for the doctor to teach a woman if possible, and if not, he should cover every part of her except the place of pain, then look, and lower his gaze from other than that place if he can, because what is established for necessity is limited to its extent.
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This is a ruling on medical treatment of a woman by a male doctor. The passage states that it is not permissible for a man to look at a woman's private parts without necessity. However, if no female doctor is available, necessity allows limited exposure. The doctor must first try to teach a woman to treat her, but if that's not possible, he may uncover only the wound site. He must lower his gaze from other areas. This follows the principle: 'Necessity is measured by its extent.' The text also cites from al-Tatarkhaniyya and al-Muhit: for childbirth, a female midwife may look at and touch the private parts of another woman.

Key Terms
ضرورة (darurah): A situation of dire need that permits what is normally prohibited.
غض البصر (ghadd al-basar): Lowering the gaze to avoid looking at prohibited things.
تقدر بقدرها (yataqaddaru bi-qadariha): It is measured by its extent' — a legal maxim meaning that permission granted due to necessity is limited to the minimum needed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Medical necessity permits a male doctor to look at a woman's wound only to the minimum extent required, and the principle 'necessity is measured by its extent' governs all such exceptions.

The Male Awrah in the Hanafi School
Definition of male awrah with degrees and bathhouse exception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of the male awrah, distinguish the degrees of prohibition for different body parts, and apply the necessity exception in bathhouse or medical contexts.

Core Idea
Male awrah: from navel to knee – navel excluded, knee included – with varying sin severity and necessity exceptions.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq fixes the precise boundaries of the male awrah and clarifies that not all parts are equal in the sin of exposure. The knee is awrah but its exposure merits a gentle rebuke, the thigh a harsh one, while the navel area is most severe. The text then enters the practical world of the public bathhouse, where necessity may allow an attendant to look at or even touch the awrah. Understanding these gradations and exceptions helps you apply the ruling with nuance in daily life.

Guided Path
1
Identify the awrah definition
Read the opening sentence: the awrah is 'what is between the navel and the knee'. Note that this is the general rule for a man looking at another man.
The author says he already covered the evidence in the book of wudu.
2
Note the inclusion and exclusion
The navel is NOT part of the awrah; the knee IS part of the awrah. This is the standard Hanafi position.
Some narrations differ on the navel, but the mufta bihi excludes it.
3
Distinguish degrees of sin
Uncovering the knee is the lightest (gentle rebuke), the thigh is more severe (harsh rebuke), and the navel area (or the area adjacent) is the most severe (striking).
The severity reflects the social and religious importance of covering.
4
Understand the bathhouse exception
A bathhouse attendant (hammami) may look at and even touch a man's awrah with his hand if there is genuine need (e.g., to pour water) provided he lowers his gaze. This is limited to necessity.
The preferred practice is for each man to handle his own awrah.
5
Recognize the disagreement on brief nudity
In a small bathhouse room, if a man needs to be naked briefly (e.g., to wring his garment), scholars differ: some permit it for a short time, others declare it sinful.
The majority view permits short exposure under necessity, but caution is recommended.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وينظر الرجل إلى الرجل إلا العورة وهي ما بين السرة والركبة، والسرة ليست من العورة والركبة منها ، وإنما لم ينبه المؤلف هنا لما قدم في كتاب الوضوء وقد بينا الدليل هناك . وحكم العورة في الركبة أخف منه في الفخذ، وفي الفخذ أخف منه في السرة حتى ينكر عليه في كشف الركبة برفق وفي الفخذ بعنف وفي السرة بضرب. وفي التتمة والإبانة : كان أبو حنيفة لا يرى بأساً بنظر الحمامي إلى عورة الرجل، وفي الكافي : وعظم الساق ليس بعورة. وفي الذخيرة وما جاز النظر إليه جاز مسه قال محمد بن مقاتل : لا بأس أن يتولى صاحب الحمام عورة إنسان بيده عند التنور إذا كان يغض بصره. قال الفقيه : وهذه في حال الضرورة لا في غيرها، وينبغي لكل إنسان أن يتولى عورته بنفسه عند التنور. وفي التتمة : البيت الصغير في الحمام يدخله الرجل يحلق عانته هل يحل له أن يكون فيه عرياناً حتى يعصر إزاره؟ فقال في المدة اليسيرة يجوز. وقال أبو الفضل : لا بأس به. وقال غيره: يأثم به
And a man looks at a man except the awrah, which is what is between the navel and the knee. The navel is not part of the awrah, and the knee is part of it. The author did not mention it here because he already covered it in the book of wudu, and we explained the evidence there. The ruling on the awrah in the knee is lighter than in the thigh, and in the thigh lighter than in the navel, such that one is rebuked mildly for uncovering the knee, harshly for the thigh, and struck for the navel. In al-Tatimma and al-Ibana: Abu Hanifa saw no harm in the bathhouse attendant looking at a man's awrah. In al-Kafi: The shin bone is not awrah. In al-Dhakhira: Whatever is permissible to look at is permissible to touch. Muhammad ibn Muqatil said: There is no harm in the bathhouse attendant handling a man's awrah with his hand near the furnace if he lowers his gaze. The faqih said: This is in cases of necessity, not otherwise, and every person should handle his own awrah near the furnace. In al-Tatimma: A small room in the bathhouse that a man enters to shave his pubic hair; is it permissible for him to be naked until he wrings his garment? He said for a short time it is permissible. Abu al-Fadl said: No harm. Others said: He sins by it.
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage defines the awrah of a man according to the Hanafi school: from the navel to the knee, with the knee included and the navel excluded. The degree of sin in exposing these parts varies: uncovering the knee is a minor offense (gentle rebuke), the thigh is more severe (harsh rebuke), and the navel (if considered awrah by some) is very severe (striking). The text then discusses exceptions: a bathhouse attendant may look at or even touch a man's awrah with his hand if necessary (e.g., to pour water) provided he lowers his gaze. But the general rule is that a person should take care of his own awrah. The ruling on being naked for a short time to wring out clothes in the bathhouse is disputed: some permit it, others declare it sinful. Note: The earlier statement that the navel is not awrah is standard Hanafi position, but the later reference to striking for uncovering it may indicate that some Hanafi scholars consider it awrah or that the strictness is for the area around it.

Key Terms
عورة (awrah): Parts of the body that must be concealed according to shariah.
حمامي (hammami): Bathhouse attendant who helps people wash.
ضرورة (darurah): Necessity that permits a normally prohibited action to the extent needed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) From the navel to the knee, including both
B) From the navel to the knee, excluding the navel but including the knee
C) Only the private parts
D) The entire lower body from waist to ankle

Key Takeaway
A man's awrah is from navel to knee (navel excluded, knee included); uncovering it is haram but graded, and necessity may permit exceptions like bathhouse attendance.

Women Looking at Women: Permissibility and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Rulings on women looking at other women with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on women looking at other women, explain the two narrations from Imam Abu Hanifa, and apply the preferred view to the case of women entering bathhouses.

Core Idea
A woman may look at another woman's body except the awrah, as between men, unless desire is feared.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies the Hanafi position on women's gaze towards other women. While the default is leniency due to homogeneity and absence of desire, an intra-school dispute exists regarding the extent of permissible looking. The preferred view equates it to the rules between men, which has practical implications such as allowing women to enter bathhouses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base rule
The text states the fourth division: woman-to-woman is like man-to-man. So the awrah between women is the same as between men (navel to knee).
This is the default analogy.
2
Note the condition of security from desire
Permissibility is conditional on being secure from desire and fitnah. If desire exists, she must lower her gaze.
The ruling is not absolute.
3
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute
There is a conflicting narration from Imam Abu Hanifa that compares woman-to-woman to man-to-mahram, restricting the back and stomach.
This shows ikhtilaf within the school.
4
Apply the tarjih mechanism
The first view is preferred (al-asahh) because it aligns with the principle that what is not awrah is not restricted, and women are naturally more shy.
Tarjih based on stronger evidence.
5
Derive the furu' ruling on bathhouses
The preferred view allows women to enter bathhouses, as the custom and need are established.
Practical application of the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله تعالى: (والمرأة للمرأة والرجل للرجل) وهذا هو القسم الرابع من التقسيمات، ومعناه المرأة للمرأة والرجل للرجل يعني نظر المرأة إلى المرأة كنظر الرجل إلى الرجل حتى يجوز للمرأة أن تنظر منها إلى ما يجوز للرجل أن ينظر إليه من الرجل إذا أمنت الشهوة والفتن لأن ما ليس بعورة لا يختلف فيه الرجال والنساء فكان لها أن تنظر منه ما ليس بعورة، وإن كان في قلبها شهوة أو أكبر رأيها أنها تشتهي أو شكت . في لها أن تغض بصرها، ولو كان الرجل هو الناظر إلى ما يجوز له منها كالوجه والكف لا ينظر إليه حتماً مع الخوف لأنه محرم عليه والفرق أن الشهوة عليهن أغلب وهي كالمتحقق حكماً فإذا اشتهى الرجل كانت الشهوة موجودة من الجانبين، وإذا اشتهت لم توجد إلا منها فكانت جانب واحد، والموجود من الجانبين أقوى في الإفضاء إلى الوقوع وإنما جاز ما ذكرنا للمجانسة وانعدام الشهوة غالباً كما في نظر الرجل إلى الرجل، وكذا الضرورة قد تحققت فيما بينهن. وعن الإمام أن تنظر المرأة إلى المرأة كنظر الرجل إلى محارمه فلا يجوز لها أن تنظر إلى الظهر والبطن في هذه الرواية بخلاف نظرها إلى الرجل لأن الرجل يحتاج إلى زيادة الانكشاف، وفي الرواية الأولى يجوز وهو الأصح ما جاز للرجل أن ينظر إليه من الرجل جاز مسه لأنه ليس بعورة ولا يخاف منه الفتنة . قال في النهاية : وهذا دليل على أنهن لا يمنعن دخول الحمام لأن العرف ظاهر به في جميع البلدان وبناء الحمامات للنساء وحاجة النساء إلى الحمام فوق حاجة الرجال لأن المقصود من دخوله الزينة والمرأة إلى هذا أحوط من الرجال ويمكن للرجل دخول الأنهار والحياض والمرأة لا تتمكن من ذلك غالباً .اهـ.
He said (may Allah have mercy on him): 'And the woman with respect to woman, and the man with respect to man.' This is the fourth division. Its meaning is that a woman looking at a woman is like a man looking at a man. So it is permissible for a woman to look at that which it is permissible for a man to look at from a man, if she is secure from desire and fitnah, because what is not awrah does not differ between men and women. So she may look at what is not awrah. But if there is desire in her heart, or she believes she desires, or she doubts, then she must lower her gaze. If a man is the one looking at what is permissible for him from her, like the face and hands, he may not look at it if there is fear, because it is prohibited for him. The difference is that desire is more dominant in women, and it is considered as existing effectively. So if a man desires, desire exists from both sides; if a woman desires, it exists only from her side, so it is one side. But existence from both sides is stronger in leading to occurrence. We permitted what we mentioned due to homogeneity and absence of desire mostly, as in a man looking at a man. Also necessity is realized among them. It is reported from the Imam that a woman looking at a woman is like a man looking at his mahrams, so she may not look at the back and stomach in this narration, contrary to her looking at a man because a man needs more exposure. In the first narration, it is permissible, and it is the most correct: whatever a man may look at from a man, he may touch because it is not awrah and no fitnah is feared. Al-Nihaya said: This is evidence that women are not prevented from entering the bathhouse because the custom is apparent in all cities and the construction of bathhouses for women, and women's need for the bathhouse is greater than men's because the purpose of entering is adornment, and women are more in need of this than men. Men can enter rivers and pools, but women cannot do that often.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on women looking at other women. The base text states: 'the woman with respect to woman, and the man with respect to man.' The commentator explains that this means the rules between women are analogous to those between men: a woman may look at another woman's body except the awrah, which for women is generally the entire body except face and hands when looking at other women? Actually, the passage says 'like a man looking at a man', meaning the awrah between women is the same as between men (navel to knee). So a woman may look at another woman's hair, arms, legs, etc., as long as she is secure from desire. If she feels desire, she must lower her gaze. There is a dispute within the Hanafi school: in one narration (from Imam Abu Hanifa), a woman looking at another woman is like a man looking at his mahram, so she cannot look at the back or stomach (i.e., more restrictions). But the preferred view is the first one, which allows looking at what a man may look at from a man. This permissibility extends to touching as well. The passage then uses this to argue that women may enter bathhouses (where they may see each other's awrah) because custom allows it and women need them for hygiene and adornment, while men have alternative options like rivers.

Key Terms
مجانسة (mujanasah): Homogeneity; being of the same gender, which reduces the likelihood of desire.
محارم (maharim): Mahrams; those whom one cannot marry due to blood relation, fosterage, or marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view in this passage, what may a woman look at from another woman?
A) Only the face and hands
B) Everything except the area between navel and knee
C) The entire body except the private parts
D) Nothing, because looking is not allowed

Key Takeaway
The default Hanafi ruling permits women to look at other women's bodies except the awrah (navel to knee), provided no desire is present; the preferred view supports leniency, allowing women to enter bathhouses.

The General Rule of Same-Gender Looking
Explains the general rule and its qiyas basis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi general rule for same-gender looking and explain its basis in qiyas.

Core Idea
Same-gender looking is permissible for non-awrah parts when safe from desire.

This lesson establishes the foundational rule: a woman looking at another woman is analogous to a man looking at a man. We will explore the reasoning (qiyas) and the key condition of safety from desire.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
State what the act is (same-gender looking) and the default ruling (mubah/permissible).
The passage begins by stating that the woman looking at a woman is like the man looking at a man.
2
Understand the Qiyas
Break down the analogy: the original case (man looking at man) has a ruling (permissible for non-awrah parts). The new case (woman looking at woman) shares the effective cause (absence of desire and similarity).
The illa is 'mujanasa' (similarity) and 'intifa' al-shahwah' (absence of desire).
3
Note the Condition of Safety from Desire
The permissibility is contingent on being safe from desire (ammina al-shahwah wa al-fitnah).
If desire is present, the ruling changes; this is developed in the next lesson.
4
Recognize the Mufta Bihi Position
The relied-upon position is that all non-awrah parts may be seen, based on the first narration.
The author states the first narration is al-asahh (most correct).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله تعالى: (والمرأة للمرأة والرجل للرجل) وهذا هو القسم الرابع من التقسيمات، ومعناه المرأة للمرأة والرجل للرجل يعني نظر المرأة إلى المرأة كنظر الرجل إلى الرجل حتى يجوز للمرأة أن تنظر منها إلى ما يجوز للرجل أن ينظر إليه من الرجل إذا أمنت الشهوة والفتن لأن ما ليس بعورة لا يختلف فيه الرجال والنساء فكان لها أن تنظر منه ما ليس بعورة، وإن كان في قلبها شهوة أو أكبر رأيها أنها تشتهي أو شكت . في لها أن تغض بصرها، ولو كان الرجل هو الناظر إلى ما يجوز له منها كالوجه والكف لا ينظر إليه حتماً مع الخوف لأنه محرم عليه والفرق أن الشهوة عليهن أغلب وهي كالمتحقق حكماً فإذا اشتهى الرجل كانت الشهوة موجودة من الجانبين، وإذا اشتهت لم توجد إلا منها فكانت جانب واحد، والموجود من الجانبين أقوى في الإفضاء إلى الوقوع وإنما جاز ما ذكرنا للمجانسة وانعدام الشهوة غالباً كما في نظر الرجل إلى الرجل، وكذا الضرورة قد تحققت فيما بينهن. وعن الإمام أن تنظر المرأة إلى المرأة كنظر الرجل إلى محارمه فلا يجوز لها أن تنظر إلى الظهر والبطن في هذه الرواية بخلاف نظرها إلى الرجل لأن الرجل يحتاج إلى زيادة الانكشاف، وفي الرواية الأولى يجوز وهو الأصح ما جاز للرجل أن ينظر إليه من الرجل جاز مسه لأنه ليس بعورة ولا يخاف منه الفتنة . قال في النهاية : وهذا دليل على أنهن لا يمنعن دخول الحمام لأن العرف ظاهر به في جميع البلدان وبناء الحمامات للنساء وحاجة النساء إلى الحمام فوق حاجة الرجال لأن المقصود من دخوله الزينة والمرأة إلى هذا أحوط من الرجال ويمكن للرجل دخول الأنهار والحياض والمرأة لا تتمكن من ذلك غالباً .اهـ. وحكي أن الإمام دخل الحمام فرأى رجلاً مكشوف العورة يقال له بطرطا وكان رجلاً متكلماً فغض أبو حنيفة بصره فقال له العاصي : مذكم أعمى الله بصرك؟ قال : مذهتك الله سترك اهـ. وفي الكافي : وعظم الساق ليس بعورة .اهـ.
He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: (And the woman for the woman and the man for the man) This is the fourth division of the divisions. Its meaning is: woman for woman and man for man, meaning the woman's looking at a woman is like the man's looking at a man, so it is permissible for a woman to look from her at what is permissible for a man to look at from a man if she is safe from desire and temptation, because what is not 'awrah does not differ between men and women, so she may look at what is not 'awrah from her. But if there is desire in her heart, or her dominant opinion is that she desires, or she doubts, then she must lower her gaze. And if a man is looking at what is permissible for him from her, like the face and hands, he does not look at it necessarily if there is fear, because it is unlawful for him. The difference is that desire is more prevalent in women, and it is considered as if it is realized in terms of ruling. So if a man desires, desire exists from both sides; if a woman desires, it exists only from her, so it is only one side. What exists from both sides is stronger in leading to occurrence. It is only permissible due to similarity and the absence of desire usually, as in a man looking at a man. Likewise, necessity is realized among them. And it is narrated from the Imam that a woman looks at a woman like a man looks at his mahrams, so she is not permitted to look at the back and belly in this narration, unlike her looking at a man because a man needs more exposure. In the first narration, it is permissible, and it is the most correct. Whatever is permissible for a man to look at from a man, it is permissible to touch, because it is not 'awrah and there is no fear of temptation from it. He said in al-Nihayah: This is evidence that women are not prevented from entering the bathhouse, because custom is manifest in all cities and the construction of bathhouses for women, and women's need for the bathhouse is greater than men's because the purpose of entering is adornment, and women are more in need of this than men, and a man can enter rivers and ponds but a woman is generally not able to do so. And it is narrated that the Imam entered the bathhouse and saw a man with exposed 'awrah called Batrta, and he was a speaking man. Abu Hanifah averted his gaze, and the disobedient one said to him: 'O effeminate one, has God blinded your eyes?' He said: 'O effeminate one, may God conceal you.' And in al-Kafi: The shin bone is not 'awrah.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is an expansion of the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding same-gender looking. The author first clarifies the general rule: a woman looking at another woman is analogous to a man looking at another man, meaning that it is permissible to look at those parts of the body that are not 'awrah, provided there is no desire or fear of temptation. The underlying reasoning ('illa) is that non-'awrah parts are the same for both genders, so there is no additional prohibition. However, if desire is present, the ruling changes: the woman must lower her gaze, and similarly a man must lower his gaze from a woman's permissible parts if he fears temptation. The author explains that the difference in treatment between men and women lies in the fact that desire is more prevalent and considered virtually present in women; if a man desires, desire is mutual, but if a woman desires, it is one-sided, and two-sided desire is more likely to lead to sin. Therefore, the permissibility is based on similarity and the usual absence of desire. Then the author mentions a narration from Imam Abu Hanifa that a woman looking at another woman is like a man looking at his mahrams, so she cannot look at the back and belly. But the first narration (permissibility) is considered the most correct (al-asahh). The passage then extends the ruling to touching: whatever is permissible to look at is also permissible to touch. Citing al-Nihayah, the author deduces that women are allowed to enter bathhouses because custom permits it and women's need is greater. The story of Imam Abu Hanifa in the bathhouse illustrates the importance of lowering the gaze even in such settings. Finally, a note from al-Kafi clarifies that the shin bone (azm al-saq) is not 'awrah.

Key Terms
الشهوة (al-shahwah): Sexual desire; its presence changes the ruling from permissible to haram.
المجانسة (al-mujanasa): Similarity of gender; it is the basis for permitting same-gender looking on analogy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the general rule in this passage, what is the primary condition for same-gender looking to be permissible?
A) The viewer must be in a state of wudu.
B) The viewer must be safe from desire and temptation.
C) The viewer must be a mahram to the person being looked at.
D) The viewer must ask permission beforehand.

Key Takeaway
Same-gender looking is permissible for non-awrah parts when the viewer is safe from desire, based on the principle of similarity and necessity.
